<p><b>Felidae</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> of <b>cats</b>. A member of this family is also called a <b>felid</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson1996_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson1996-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Living cats belong to the <a href="/wiki/Subfamily" title="Subfamily">subfamilies</a>:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pantherinae" title="Pantherinae">Pantherinae</a> – comprising the <a href="/wiki/Tiger" title="Tiger">tiger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jaguar" title="Jaguar">jaguar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leopard" title="Leopard">leopard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snow_leopard" title="Snow leopard">snow leopard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clouded_leopard" title="Clouded leopard">clouded leopard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunda_clouded_leopard" title="Sunda clouded leopard">Sunda clouded leopard</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson1996_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson1996-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Felinae" title="Felinae">Felinae</a> – including all the non-pantherine cats<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Cats are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obligate_carnivores" title="Obligate carnivores">obligate carnivores</a>; they must consume meat to survive. They are sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Hypercarnivore" title="Hypercarnivore">hypercarnivores</a> because of the very large proportion of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> they require in their diet, much more than most other mammals. Of the 13 terrestrial families in the <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">Carnivora</a>, they are the strictest <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first cats emerged during the <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a>, about 25 million years ago. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric" title="Prehistoric">prehistoric</a> times, there was a third subfamily, the <a href="/wiki/Machairodontinae" title="Machairodontinae">Machairodontinae</a>, which included the <a href="/wiki/Saber-toothed_cat" title="Saber-toothed cat">saber-toothed cats</a>, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Smilodon" title="Smilodon">Smilodon</a></i>. Other superficially cat-like mammals, such as the <a href="/wiki/Metatheria" title="Metatheria">metatherians</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Thylacosmilus" title="Thylacosmilus">Thylacosmilus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Thylacoleo" title="Thylacoleo">Thylacoleo</a></i>, or the <a href="/wiki/Barbourofelidae" title="Barbourofelidae">Barbourofelidae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nimravidae" title="Nimravidae">Nimravidae</a>, are not included in Felidae despite <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">superficial similarities</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Felidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The 41 known cat species (40 if the <a href="/wiki/Iriomote_cat" title="Iriomote cat">Iriomote cat</a> is considered to be only a subspecies of the <a href="/wiki/Leopard_cat" title="Leopard cat">Leopard cat</a>) in the world today are all descended from a common ancestor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-1">[1]</a></sup> Cats originated in Asia and spread across <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a> by crossing <a href="/wiki/Land_bridge" title="Land bridge">land bridges</a>. Testing of <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_DNA" title="Nuclear DNA">nuclear DNA</a> revealed that the ancient cats evolved into eight main lineages that diverged in the course of at least 10 migrations (in both directions) from continent to continent via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bering_land_bridge" title="Bering land bridge">Bering land bridge</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama" title="Isthmus of Panama">Isthmus of Panama</a>, with the <i><a href="/wiki/Panthera" title="Panthera">Panthera</a></i> genus being the oldest and the <i><a href="/wiki/Felis" title="Felis">Felis</a></i> genus being the youngest. About 60% of the modern cat species are estimated to have developed within the last million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson06_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson06-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Felidae's closest relatives are thought to be the <a href="/wiki/Asiatic_linsang" title="Asiatic linsang">Asiatic linsangs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Together with the <a href="/wiki/Viverridae" title="Viverridae">Viverridae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyena" title="Hyena">hyenas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongoose" title="Mongoose">mongooses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eupleridae" title="Eupleridae">Madagascar carnivores</a>, they form the suborder <a href="/wiki/Feliformia" title="Feliformia">Feliformia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-convergence_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-convergence-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most cat species share a genetic anomaly that prevents them from tasting sweetness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLOS_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-PLOS-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most cat species have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haploid" title="Haploid">haploid</a> number of 18 or 19. New World cats (those in Central and South America) have a haploid number of 18, possibly due to the combination of two smaller <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a> into a larger one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robinson_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-robinson-13">[13]</a></sup> Prior to this discovery, biologists had been largely unable to establish a family tree of cats from <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> records because the fossils of different cat species all look very much alike, differing primarily in size.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Domestic cats may either have a long or short tail. At one point, biologists had to consider whether the short tail also found in the lynx was the ancestral or derived trait. Without looking at the fossil record, researchers were able to look at the character states found in their outgroups. Because all animals belonging to Felidae’s sister taxa, Viverridae, have long tails, scientists could infer that this character state represents the ancestral trait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-convergence_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-convergence-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some domestic cats display a rosette pattern on their coats. This character state, however, is not related to the rosettes found on big cats. Domestic cats and big cats underwent convergent evolution for this trait. The most common ancestor to all cats had a flecked coat. Lynxes display this character state. The jaguarundi lost this character state secondarily. The most common recent ancestor of snow leopards, tigers, jaguars, lions, and leopards developed a coat with rosette patterns from the flecked patterns. Tigers and lions, however, do not display rosettes as adults. They both have lost this ancestral character state over time. Adult tigers actually display elongated rosettes that now appear as stripes. Adult lions seem to lack any distinctive markings altogether. Both juvenile tigers and lions, however, display partial rosettes. This ancestral character state appears only during these early stages, supporting the notion that ontogeny reflects phylogeny. The rosette patterns found on snow leopards, jaguars, and leopards all have a common origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carnivores compete against each other. There is fossil evidence that felids have been more successful than <a href="/wiki/Canidae" title="Canidae">canids</a> in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Felidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Cats are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obligate_carnivores" title="Obligate carnivores">obligate carnivores</a>, requiring a diet of meat and organs to survive. Aside from the <a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lion</a>, wild felids are generally solitary; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feral" title="Feral">feral</a> domestic cats do, however, form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feral_cat_colony" title="Feral cat colony">colonies</a>. Cheetah males are also known to live and hunt in groups. Felids are generally secretive animals, often nocturnal, and live in relatively inaccessible habitats. Around three-quarters of cat species live in <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forested</a> terrain, and they are generally agile climbers. However, felids may be found in almost any environment, with some species being native to <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountainous</a> terrain or <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">deserts</a>.</p>
<p>Wild felids are native to every continent except <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_appearance">Physical appearance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Felidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Physical appearance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Smilodon01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="833" data-file-width="919" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Smilodon01.jpg/220px-Smilodon01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Smilodon01.jpg/330px-Smilodon01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Smilodon01.jpg/440px-Smilodon01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Smilodon01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Skull of the machairodontine <i><a href="/wiki/Smilodon" title="Smilodon">Smilodon</a></i> (reconstruction)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Panthera_leo_Kruger_Skull.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1228" data-file-width="1523" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Panthera_leo_Kruger_Skull.jpg/220px-Panthera_leo_Kruger_Skull.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Panthera_leo_Kruger_Skull.jpg/330px-Panthera_leo_Kruger_Skull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Panthera_leo_Kruger_Skull.jpg/440px-Panthera_leo_Kruger_Skull.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Panthera_leo_Kruger_Skull.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lion skull</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Felids tend to have lithe and flexible bodies with muscular limbs. In the great majority of species, the tail is between a third and a half of the body length, although with some exceptions (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Bobcat" title="Bobcat">bobcat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Margay" title="Margay">margay</a>). The limbs are <a href="/wiki/Digitigrade" title="Digitigrade">digitigrade</a> with soft toe pads and protractible claws. Compared with most other mammals, the head is highly domed with a short muzzle. The skull possesses wide <a href="/wiki/Zygomatic_arch" title="Zygomatic arch">zygomatic arches</a> and a large <a href="/wiki/Sagittal_crest" title="Sagittal crest">sagittal crest</a>, both of which allow for the attachment of strong jaw muscles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCoW_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The various species of felids vary greatly in size. One of the smallest is the <a href="/wiki/Black-footed_cat" title="Black-footed cat">black-footed cat</a>, measuring 35 to 40 cm (14 to 16 in) long, while the largest in the wild is the <a href="/wiki/Tiger" title="Tiger">tiger</a>, which can grow up to 350 cm (11.5 ft) in length<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-der-tiger_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-der-tiger-18">[18]</a></sup> and weigh 300 kilograms (660 lb).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Felid <a href="/wiki/Fur" title="Fur">fur</a> takes many different forms, being much thicker in those species living in cold environments, such as the <a href="/wiki/Snow_leopard" title="Snow leopard">snow leopard</a>. The color of felids is also highly variable—although brown to golden fur is common in most species—usually marked with distinctive spots, stripes, or <a href="/wiki/Rosette_(zoology)" title="Rosette (zoology)">rosettes</a>. The only felids to lack significant markings are the lion, <a href="/wiki/Cougar" title="Cougar">puma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caracal" title="Caracal">caracal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jaguarundi" title="Jaguarundi">jaguarundi</a>. Many species exhibit <a href="/wiki/Melanism" title="Melanism">melanism</a>, in which some individuals have an all-black coat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCoW_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tongue" title="Tongue">tongue</a> of felids is covered with horny <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filiform_papilla" title="Filiform papilla">papillae</a>, which rasp meat from prey and aid in grooming.</p>
<p>All felids have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protraction" title="Protraction">protractible</a> claws that can protract from a retracted, at-rest position. In a few species, such as the <a href="/wiki/Cheetah" title="Cheetah">cheetah</a>, the claws remain visible even when at rest (retracted). The claws are retracted when the animal is relaxed and protracted when they are in use. They are attached to the terminal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phalanges" title="Phalanges">bone of the toe</a> with a tough <a href="/wiki/Ligament" title="Ligament">ligament</a>; when the animal contracts muscles in the toe to straighten it, the ligament forces the claw outwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCoW_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-17">[17]</a></sup> Cats have five toes on their forefeet and four on their hindfeet, reflecting their reliance on gripping and holding down their prey with their claws.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In the Felidae, the <a href="/wiki/Baculum" title="Baculum">baculum</a> is shorter than in the <a href="/wiki/Canidae" title="Canidae">Canidae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ewer1973_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ewer1973-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Senses">Senses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Felidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Senses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="500" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_1.png/220px-MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_1.png/330px-MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_1.png/440px-MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_1.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Comparative illustration of cats of the genus <i>Panthera</i> and <i>Felis</i> by N. N. Kondakov</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="471" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_2.png/220px-MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_2.png/330px-MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_2.png/440px-MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_2.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MSU_V2P2_-_Panthera_vs_Felis_2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Feeding postures of <i>Panthera</i> and <i>Felis</i>, as illustrated by N. N. Kondakov</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Felids have relatively large eyes, situated to provide <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">binocular vision</a>. Their night vision is especially good due to the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Tapetum_lucidum" title="Tapetum lucidum">tapetum lucidum</a>, which reflects light back inside the eyeball, and gives felid eyes their distinctive shine. As a result, the eyes of felids are about six times more light sensitive than those of humans, and many species are at least partially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nocturnal" title="Nocturnal">nocturnal</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a> of felids also contains a relatively high proportion of <a href="/wiki/Rod_cell" title="Rod cell">rod cells</a>, adapted for distinguishing moving objects in conditions of dim light, which are complemented by the presence of <a href="/wiki/Cone_cell" title="Cone cell">cone cells</a> for sensing color during the day. However, felids appear to have relatively poor color vision in comparison with humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCoW_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-17">[17]</a></sup> This is explained by the fact that felids see moving objects more "colorfully" than still objects, but in an intact environment, are unable to distinguish color tones alone (like turquoise compared to teal, for example).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pinna_(anatomy)" title="Pinna (anatomy)">external ears</a> of felids are also large, and especially sensitive to high-frequency sounds in the smaller cats. This sensitivity allows them to locate small <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodent</a> prey; cats do not apparently produce such sounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCoW_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Felids also have a highly developed sense of smell, although not to the degree seen in <a href="/wiki/Canidae" title="Canidae">canids</a>; this is further supplemented by the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Vomeronasal_organ" title="Vomeronasal organ">vomeronasal organ</a> in the roof of the mouth, allowing the animal to "taste" the air. The use of this organ is associated with the <a href="/wiki/Flehmen_response" title="Flehmen response">Flehmen response</a>, in which the upper lip is curled upwards. Most felids are unable to taste sweetness due to a mutated gene in their taste buds. Exceptions include members of the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Leopardus" title="Leopardus">Leopardus</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otocolobus" title="Otocolobus">Otocolobus</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Felids possess highly sensitive <a href="/wiki/Whiskers" title="Whiskers">whiskers</a> set deep within the skin, which provide the cat with sensory information about the slightest air movement around it. Whiskers are very helpful to nocturnal hunters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Most felids are able to land on their feet after a fall, an ability that relies on vision, body flexibility and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equilibrioception" title="Equilibrioception">sense of balance</a> acting together.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dentition">Dentition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Felidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Dentition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Felids have a relatively small number of teeth compared with other carnivorans, a feature associated with their short muzzles. With a few exceptions, such as the <a href="/wiki/Lynx" title="Lynx">lynx</a>, they have the <a href="/wiki/Dentition" title="Dentition">dental formula</a>: <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;" title="upper dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">3.1.3.1</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;" title="lower dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">3.1.2.1</span></span></span>. The <a href="/wiki/Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth">canine teeth</a> are large, reaching exceptional size in the extinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saber-tooth_cat" title="Saber-tooth cat">saber-tooth</a> species. The upper third premolar and lower molar are adapted as <a href="/wiki/Carnassial" title="Carnassial">carnassial</a> teeth, suited to tearing and cutting flesh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCoW_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Felid jaws can only move vertically, which prevents them from being able to <a href="/wiki/Mastication" title="Mastication">chew efficiently</a>, but makes it easier for their powerful <a href="/wiki/Masseter_muscle" title="Masseter muscle">masseter</a> jaw muscles to hold struggling prey.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocalisations">Vocalisations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Felidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Vocalisations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All felids share a broadly similar set of vocalisations, but with some variation between species. In particular, the pitch of calls varies, with larger species producing deeper sounds; overall, the frequency of felid calls ranges between 50 and 10,000 hertz.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>All felids are able to spit, hiss, <a href="/wiki/Growling" title="Growling">growl</a>, snarl, and mew. The first four sounds are all used in an aggressive context. The spitting sound is a sudden burst, typically used when making threats, especially towards other species. The hiss is a prolonged, atonal sound used in close range to other members of the species, when the animal is uncertain whether to attack or retreat. Growling is used to indicate a willingness to attack, while the higher-pitched snarl is used when adopting a defensive posture.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The mewing sound may be used either as a close-contact call, typically between a mother and kittens, or as a louder, longer distance call, primarily during the mating season. The acoustic properties of the mew vary somewhat between different felid species; extreme examples include the whistling sound made by cougars and the mew-grunt of lions and tigers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Most felids seem to be able to <a href="/wiki/Purr" title="Purr">purr</a>, vibrating the muscles in their <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">larynx</a> to produce a distinctive buzzing sound. In the wild, purring is used while a mother is caring for kittens. Precisely which species of felids are able to purr is a matter of debate, but the sound has been recorded in most of the smaller species, as well as the cheetah and cougar, and may also be found in the <a href="/wiki/Big_cat" title="Big cat">big cats</a>.</p>
<p>Other common felid vocalisations include the gurgle, wah-wah, <a href="/wiki/Prusten" title="Prusten">prusten</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roar_(utterance)" title="Roar (utterance)">roar</a>. The first two sounds are found only among the Felinae (small cats). Gurgling is a quiet sound used during meetings between friendly individuals, as well as during courtship and when nursing kittens. The wah-wah is a short, deep-sounding call used in close contact, and is not found in all species (it is, for example, absent in the domestic cat).</p>
<p>In contrast, prusten and roaring are found only in big cats. Prusten is a short, soft, snorting sound reported in tigers, jaguars, snow leopards, and clouded leopards; it is used during contact between friendly individuals. The roar is an especially loud call with a distinctive pattern that depends on the species. The ability to roar comes from an elongated and specially adapted larynx and <a href="/wiki/Hyoid_bone" title="Hyoid bone">hyoid apparatus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> When air passes through the larynx on the way from the lungs, the cartilage walls of the larynx vibrate, producing sound. Only lions, leopards, tigers, and jaguars are truly able to roar, although the loudest mews of snow leopards have a similar, if less structured, sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WCoW_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-17">[17]</a></sup> Tigers and jaguars have a very snarly roar, while the roar of leopards and lions is much more throaty.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_territorial_behavior">Social and territorial behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Felidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Social and territorial behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Territorial_marking">Territorial marking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Felidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Territorial marking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Within the Felidae, male felids can urinate backwards by curving the tip of the glans penis backward. Urine marking by felids is also known as "spray-urinating" or "spray-marking". To identify their territories, male tigers mark trees by spraying urine and anal gland secretions, as well as marking trails with scat. Males show a grimacing face, called the Flehmen response, when identifying a female's reproductive condition by sniffing their urine markings.</p>
<p>Lions use urine to mark their territories. They often scrape the ground while urinating, and the urine often flows in short spurts, instead of flowing continuously. They often urinate on vegetation, or on tree trunks at least one meter high. Male lions spray 1–20 jets of urine at an angle of 20–30 degrees upward, at a range of up to 4 meters behind them.</p>
<p>Male cheetahs mark their territory by urinating on objects that stand out, such as trees, logs, or termite mounds. The whole coalition contributes to the scent. Males will attempt to kill any intruders, and fights result in serious injury or death. When male cheetahs urine-mark their territories, they stand a meter away from a tree or rock surface with the tail raised, pointing the penis either horizontally backward or 60° upward. The odor of cheetah urine (unlike that of other large felids) cannot be easily detected by humans.</p>
<p>Black-footed cats use scent marking throughout their ranges, with males spraying urine up to 12 times an hour.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Felidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Traditionally, five subfamilies have been distinguished within the Felidae based on <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotypical</a> features: the <a href="/wiki/Felinae" title="Felinae">Felinae</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pantherinae" title="Pantherinae">Pantherinae</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acinonychinae" title="Acinonychinae">Acinonychinae</a> (cheetahs), the extinct <a href="/wiki/Machairodontinae" title="Machairodontinae">Machairodontinae</a>, and the extinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proailurinae" title="Proailurinae">Proailurinae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKenna_.26_Bell_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-McKenna_.26_Bell-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Felidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Phylogeny">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">Molecular phylogenetic analysis</a> suggests that living (extant) felids fall into eight lineages (clades).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson06_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson06-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson97_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson97-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson05_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson05-22">[22]</a></sup> In 2006, Johnson et al. presented the following cladogram (the genera shown are those included in the analysis):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson06_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson06-9">[9]</a></sup></p>

<tr style="background-color:#ffbbdd;color:#882277;text-align:right;font-weight:bold;padding:1px 10px 10px 1px;">
<td colspan="2">
<p>Domestic cat lineage    </p>
</td>
<td></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;"><br/></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;"><br/></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;"><br/></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;"><br/></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;"><br/></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;"><br/></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;"><br/></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><br/>
The placement of the cheetah within the Puma lineage invalidates the traditional subfamily Acinonychinae, and recent sources use only two subfamilies for extant genera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-1">[1]</a></sup> The eight lineages divide between these as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Subfamily <a href="/wiki/Pantherinae" title="Pantherinae">Pantherinae</a>:
<ul>
<li>Lineage 1 (Panthera lineage): <i><a href="/wiki/Panthera" title="Panthera">Panthera</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Neofelis" title="Neofelis">Neofelis</a></i></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Subfamily <a href="/wiki/Felinae" title="Felinae">Felinae</a>:
<ul>
<li>Lineage 2 (Bay cat lineage): <i><a href="/wiki/Pardofelis" title="Pardofelis">Pardofelis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Catopuma" title="Catopuma">Catopuma</a></i></li>
<li>Lineage 3 (Caracal lineage): <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leptailurus" title="Leptailurus">Leptailurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Caracal" title="Caracal">Caracal</a></i> (including <i>Profelis</i>)</li>
<li>Lineage 4 (Ocelot cat lineage): <i><a href="/wiki/Leopardus" title="Leopardus">Leopardus</a></i></li>
<li>Lineage 5 (Lynx lineage): <i><a href="/wiki/Lynx" title="Lynx">Lynx</a></i></li>
<li>Lineage 6 (Puma lineage): <i><a href="/wiki/Puma_(genus)" title="Puma (genus)">Puma</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Acinonyx" title="Acinonyx">Acinonyx</a></i></li>
<li>Lineage 7 (Leopard cat lineage): <i><a href="/wiki/Prionailurus" title="Prionailurus">Prionailurus</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otocolobus" title="Otocolobus">Otocolobus</a></i></li>
<li>Lineage 8 (Domestic cat lineage): <i><a href="/wiki/Felis" title="Felis">Felis</a></i></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The last four lineages are more related to each other than to any of the first four, so form a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> within the Felinae subfamily of family Felidae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson06_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson06-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extant_species">Extant species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Felidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Extant species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

It includes all of the currently living species of cats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-1">[1]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_felids">Fossil felids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Felidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fossil felids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Possibly the oldest known true felid (<i><a href="/wiki/Proailurus" title="Proailurus">Proailurus</a></i>) lived in the late <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> epochs. During the Miocene, it gave way to <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudaelurus" title="Pseudaelurus">Pseudaelurus</a></i>. <i>Pseudaelurus</i> is believed to be the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latest_common_ancestor" title="Latest common ancestor">latest common ancestor</a> of the two extant subfamilies and the extinct subfamily, <a href="/wiki/Machairodontinae" title="Machairodontinae">Machairodontinae</a>. This group, better known as the saber-tooth cats, became extinct in the Late <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> era. The group includes the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Smilodon" title="Smilodon">Smilodon</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Machairodus" title="Machairodus">Machairodus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Homotherium" title="Homotherium">Homotherium</a></i>. The <a href="/wiki/Metailurini" title="Metailurini">Metailurini</a> were originally classified as a distinct tribe within the Machairodontinae, though they count as members of the Felinae in recent times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fossils_explained_52_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fossils_explained_52-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Most extinct cat-like animals, once regarded as members of the Felidae, later turned out to be members of related, but distinct, families: the "false sabretooths" <a href="/wiki/Nimravidae" title="Nimravidae">Nimravidae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barbourofelidae" title="Barbourofelidae">Barbourofelidae</a>. As a result, sabretooth "cats" seem to belong to four different lineages. The total number of fossil felids known to science is low compared to other carnivoran families, such as <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bears</a>. Felidae radiated quite recently and most of the extant species are relatively young.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_genera">Fossil genera</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Felidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Fossil genera">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Panthera_leo_atrox_Sergiodlarosa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="546" data-file-width="836" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Panthera_leo_atrox_Sergiodlarosa.jpg/220px-Panthera_leo_atrox_Sergiodlarosa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Panthera_leo_atrox_Sergiodlarosa.jpg/330px-Panthera_leo_atrox_Sergiodlarosa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Panthera_leo_atrox_Sergiodlarosa.jpg/440px-Panthera_leo_atrox_Sergiodlarosa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Panthera_leo_atrox_Sergiodlarosa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/American_lion" title="American lion">American lion</a> was one of the abundant <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene_megafauna" title="Pleistocene megafauna">Pleistocene megafauna</a>, a wide variety of very large mammals that became extinct about 10,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Megantereon_model.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Megantereon_model.jpg/220px-Megantereon_model.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Megantereon_model.jpg/330px-Megantereon_model.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Megantereon_model.jpg/440px-Megantereon_model.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Megantereon_model.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Megantereon" title="Megantereon">Megantereon</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The list follows McKenna and Bell's <i>Classification of Mammals</i> for prehistoric genera. <i>Pseudaelurus</i> is included in the Felinae as per McKenna &amp; Bell, despite its basal position in felid evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKenna_.26_Bell_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-McKenna_.26_Bell-2">[2]</a></sup> Inconsistent with McKenna and Bell, three additional prehistoric genera, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miracinonyx" title="Miracinonyx">Miracinonyx</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lokontailurus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lokontailurus (page does not exist)">Lokontailurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Xenosmilus" title="Xenosmilus">Xenosmilus</a></i>, are listed. <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sivapanthera&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sivapanthera (page does not exist)">Sivapanthera</a></i> is included in the Felinae (not Acinonychinae) and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ischrosmilus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ischrosmilus (page does not exist)">Ischrosmilus</a></i> is included in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Smilodon" title="Smilodon">Smilodon</a></i>.</p>
<ul>
<li>†<b>Proailurinae</b>
<ul>
<li>†<i><a href="/wiki/Proailurus" title="Proailurus">Proailurus</a></i></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b>Felinae</b>
<ul>
<li>†<i><a href="/wiki/Pseudaelurus" title="Pseudaelurus">Pseudaelurus</a></i></li>

<th class="navbox-group" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Eared_seal" title="Eared seal">Otariidae</a><br/>

<small><span style="color:#696969"><span style="font-weight:normal;">(<a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">Pinniped</a> inclusive)</span></span></small></th>

<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/South_American_fur_seal" title="South American fur seal">South American fur seal (<i>A. australis</i>)</a></li>
